### 6.8 "Spanish Grandeur in Mississippi"

**Cover:** `assets/images/magazines/OA-2001-41-cover.jpg` — `MAG-015` (Oxford American #41 Fall 2001 - Spanish Grandeur in Mississippi)


Venue: Oxford American, Issue 41 (Fall 2001). [The issue number is sometimes mistakenly given as "30" or the year as "Fall 2000"; the correct citation is Issue 41, Fall 2001, confirmed by the primary URL https://oxfordamerican.org/magazine/issue-41-fall-2001.]

Column classification: "Art Views" (regular OA column). Full TOC entry: "Art Views: Spanish Grandeur in Mississippi / by Donna Tartt." This places the piece as visual-art criticism. The issue's thematic focus is a tribute to Eudora Welty (who died April 23, 2001); Tartt contributes alongside John Updike, Bobbie Ann Mason, X.J. Kennedy (Welty tributes), and essayists Paul Reyes and Jacob Levenson. Given the Art Views column designation and the "Spanish Grandeur" title, the piece likely concerns Spanish colonial/European architectural heritage or visual art traditions in Mississippi (e.g., the grand Spanish-influenced houses of Natchez).

OA back-issue note: Physical copies of Issue 41 are sold in the OA Goods "Donna Tartt Collection" bundle (Issues 2, 6, and 41 together).

![Fig. 6.8 — Oxford American, Issue 41, Fall 2001. Cover of the issue containing Tartt's "Spanish Grandeur in Mississippi."](assets/images/magazines/OA-2001-41-cover.jpg "Fig. 6.8 — Oxford American Issue 41, Fall 2001")

*Fig. 6.8 — Oxford American, Issue 41, Fall 2001 — contains Tartt's "Spanish Grandeur in Mississippi" (Art Views column).*

**Canonical URL:** https://oxfordamerican.org/magazine/issue-41-fall-2001 (OA Issue 41 landing page; no individual article page exists on the OA website)
**Text status:** Print only — no individual article page exists on the OA website; the issue TOC confirms the Tartt contribution but full text requires a physical copy of OA Issue 41.
**Wayback note:** Unlike Issues 6 and 29, the Wayback Machine could not independently corroborate the Tartt byline for Issue 41 — the captures of the OA Issue 41 landing page are empty client-side-rendered SPA shells. Files saved locally at `assets/sources/archive/wayback/OA-Issue41-2001-TOC-PREFIX-...-WB20210419223213.html` and `OA-Issue41-2001-TOC-LARGER-WB20250619231741.html`. The Tartt byline for "Spanish Grandeur in Mississippi" rests on the OA Goods listing of Issue 41 inside the "Donna Tartt Collection" three-issue bundle and the Wikipedia bibliography entry. A physical copy would provide definitive confirmation.
**Cover image:** assets/images/magazines/OA-2001-41-cover.jpg (Fig. 6.8 — Oxford American Issue 41, Fall 2001)

### 6.9 "In Melbourne"

**Cover:** `assets/images/magazines/OA-1995-06-cover.jpg` — `MAG-016` (Oxford American #6 Mar/Apr 1995 - In Melbourne)

Venue: Oxford American, Issue 6 (March/April 1995). URL: https://oxfordamerican.org/magazine/issue-6-march-april-1995.

Section classification: "Essays" (per OA issue TOC). Full TOC entry: "Essays: In Melbourne / by Donna Tartt."

Probable nature of piece: The title and "Essays" classification suggest a personal travel essay about Melbourne, Australia. Tartt attended the Adelaide Writers' Week in early 1994 as a last-minute replacement for Gore Vidal (confirmed in a Sydney Morning Herald interview, November 2, 2002). The March/April 1995 publication date, roughly 12 months after a confirmed Australian visit, fits the timeline for a personal essay drafted in the aftermath of that trip. The piece is listed alongside essays by Lewis Nordan and a Willie Morris profile in an issue that also features previously-unpublished Zora Neale Hurston fiction.

OA back-issue note: Physical copies of Issue 6 are sold in the OA Goods "Donna Tartt Collection" bundle (Issues 2, 6, and 41 together).

![Fig. 6.9 — Oxford American, Issue 6, March/April 1995. Cover of the issue containing Tartt's "In Melbourne."](assets/images/magazines/OA-1995-06-cover.jpg "Fig. 6.9 — Oxford American Issue 6, March/April 1995")

*Fig. 6.9 — Oxford American, Issue 6, March/April 1995 — contains Tartt's "In Melbourne" (Essays section).*

Collector note: A physical copy is needed to confirm page range and full content. OA Issue 6 back copies are available via OA Goods.

**Canonical URL:** https://oxfordamerican.org/magazine/issue-6-march-april-1995 (OA Issue 6 landing page; no individual article page exists on the OA website)
**Text status:** Print only — no individual article page exists on the OA website; the issue TOC confirms the Tartt contribution but full text requires a physical copy of OA Issue 6.
**Wayback corroboration:** OA Issue 6 landing page TOC archived in the Wayback Machine. Capture saved locally at `assets/sources/archive/wayback/OA-Issue6-1995-TOC-PREFIX-...issue-6-march-april-1995-WB20210419230542.html` (14 KB). The capture renders the byline list: *"Essays by Donna Tartt and Lewis Nordan. 'My Friend Forrest Gump' by Willie Morris."* and the TOC line *"ESSAYS — IN MELBOURNE by Donna Tartt — THE MAKING OF A BOOK by Lewis Nordan."* The article body was not captured.
**Cover image:** assets/images/magazines/OA-1995-06-cover.jpg (Fig. 6.9 — Oxford American Issue 6, Mar/Apr 1995)

### 6.10 "Early Times in Mississippi" (in "Ode to Bourbon")

**Cover:** `assets/images/magazines/GG-2007-HOLIDAY-cover.jpg` — `MAG-017` (Garden & Gun Holiday 2007 - Early Times in Mississippi)

Venue: Garden & Gun magazine, Holiday 2007 issue. Collective feature title: "Ode to Bourbon: Four writers' sweet reflection on a sour mash," by Donna Tartt, John Currence, John T. Edge, and Roy Blount Jr. Tartt's individual contribution is titled "EARLY TIMES IN MISSISSIPPI: A look at the spirit through the prism of the past." URL: https://gardenandgun.com/ode-to-bourbon.

Content summary: A personal memoir essay recalling bourbon in Tartt's Mississippi childhood — Christmas parties at her grandmother's, growing up in a dry county, "Dr. Brown's Package Store" just across the Yalobusha County line, her first date, bourbon as a homesickness cure at school in New England, and eventually bourbon as "an unbearably sad drink" — the smell of funerals and lost friends. One of the richest autobiographical essays Tartt has published outside her novels.

![Fig. 6.10 — Garden & Gun, Holiday 2007. Cover of the issue containing Tartt's "Early Times in Mississippi."](assets/images/magazines/GG-2007-HOLIDAY-cover.jpg "Fig. 6.10 — Garden & Gun Holiday 2007")

*Fig. 6.10 — Garden & Gun, Holiday 2007 — contains Tartt's "Early Times in Mississippi" (part of the "Ode to Bourbon" feature).*

Bibliographic detail: Garden & Gun, Holiday 2007 (the magazine's inaugural holiday issue — G&G launched in 2007). Issue confirmed via https://gardenandgun.com/issue/holiday-07. Other contributors to the bourbon feature: Roy Blount Jr. ("Tennessee Sipping Whiskey"), John T. Edge ("Respecting Reserve"), John Currence ("Proud to Drink American"). Context note: the article blurb identifies Tartt as "a Mississippi native, now at work on her third novel from the quiet of rural Virginia" — confirming this was written during the long composition of The Goldfinch (published 2013).

Collector significance: G&G Holiday 2007 is among the earliest issues of this now-prestigious Southern magazine (founded 2007). Back copies are increasingly scarce. This is the only confirmed Tartt contribution to Garden & Gun.

**Canonical URL:** https://gardenandgun.com/ode-to-bourbon (Garden & Gun; full text freely available online)
**Text status:** Full text saved at `assets/sources/archive/GardenAndGun-2007Holiday-OdeToBourbon-Tartt-Currence-Edge-Blount.html` (~4,170 words across the four-author feature; Tartt's "Early Times in Mississippi" section comprises roughly 1,200 of those words and is the lead piece). G&G metadata block confirms publication date: post_date 2007-11-07, issue "Holiday 07," credited authors "Donna Tartt, John Currence, John T. Edge, and Roy Blount Jr."
**Cover image:** assets/images/magazines/GG-2007-HOLIDAY-cover.jpg (Fig. 6.10 — Garden & Gun, Holiday 2007)

**Anthology reprint:** "Early Times in Mississippi" was reprinted in *Cornbread Nation 5: The Best of Southern Food Writing*, ed. Fred W. Sauceman, gen. ed. John T. Edge (UGA Press / Southern Foodways Alliance, 2014; ISBN 9780820335070), at **pp. 275–277**. Reprint provenance confirmed via the volume's source-credit page (see §7.21 for full bibliographic detail). Full reprinted text archived at `assets/sources/archive/excerpts/Tartt-EarlyTimesInMississippi-CornbreadNation5-2014-pp275-277.md`.

### 6.11 "This much I know"

**Cover:** `assets/images/MAG-018_OMObserver_2003-11-16_cover_QnIAAOSw.webp` — `MAG-018` (OM — Observer Magazine, Nov 16 2003 - "Stocking thrillers" gift-guide cover; contains Tartt's "This Much I Know" interior column)

Venue: The Observer (London, Greater London, England), Sunday, November 16, 2003. Section: "UP FRONT THIS MUCH I KNOW." Full dateline: "Donna Tartt, Author, 39, Stockholm." Pages 217–218 of the issue.

Full bibliographic detail: The Observer (London), Sunday, November 16, 2003. Section: "UP FRONT THIS MUCH I KNOW." Byline: Donna Tartt. Interview conducted by: Dee O'Connell. Photograph by: Ulla Montan. Footer line: "Donna Tartt's The Little Friend is published by Bloomsbury, £7.99." The column runs on p. 217 (full text) and p. 218 (full-page portrait photograph of Tartt, standing outdoors at night).

Content: reflections on travelling alone in France with her dog; attachment to coats (a cashmere coat bought 10 years earlier); media shyness / dislike of publicity; the Gramercy Park Hotel as a second home; waking up not knowing what city she is in; disappointment at finding she could not compose at a typewriter; still writing in messy little notebooks she has used since age six; Oscar Wilde's deathbed wallpaper remark; New York as the only city; being a cheerleader (corroborates "Basketball Season"); dogs stopping to speak to you; writers dressing as dandies or in rags; losing notebooks constantly; trying to be disciplined by nature but not; never having seen Sex and the City.

Context: Published in November 2003 during the transatlantic publicity tour for The Little Friend (Bloomsbury UK pub. 2002; US Knopf pub. October 22, 2002). The Stockholm dateline confirms a Scandinavian leg of that tour. The Observer's "This much I know" series ran regular celebrity interview-columns of this format.

Collector significance: The Observer, November 16, 2003 Sunday issue is the primary periodical record (pages 217–218). Observer back issues are increasingly difficult to source in fine condition. This appears to be Tartt's only confirmed contribution to The Observer.

**Canonical URL:** Print only — The Observer, November 16, 2003 (pp. 217–218); primary scans in assets/articles/
**Text status:** Print only — no confirmed online archive; primary newspaper scans held locally.
**Cover image:** assets/images/MAG-018_OMObserver_2003-11-16_cover_QnIAAOSw.webp — front cover of OM (Observer Magazine supplement), 16 November 2003 ("Stocking thrillers" Christmas gift-guide special; the Tartt "This Much I Know" column runs on the supplement's interior pp. 217–218).

### 6.12 "Willie Morris (1934–1999)" [Tribute]

**Cover:** `assets/images/magazines/OA-1999-29-cover.jpg` — `MAG-019` (Oxford American #29 Sep/Oct 1999 - Willie Morris Tribute)

Venue: Oxford American, Issue 29 (September/October 1999). Section: "Tribute." Full TOC entry: "Tribute: Willie Morris (1934-1999) / by Donna Tartt." Running alongside a second tribute, "It Cannot Be Long: Remembering a friend" by William Styron. A photograph by Maude Schuyler Clay of Willie Morris in Jackson, Mississippi, 1993 accompanies the tributes.

Nature of piece: A memorial tribute to Willie Morris (January 2, 1934 — August 2, 1999), Tartt's mentor and the person who first recognised her talent at the University of Mississippi. Morris introduced himself to her with the words "My name is Willie Morris and I think you're a genius" — a story Tartt has retold in several interviews. Morris served as a friend and mentor for years and was an Oxford American contributor himself. His death on August 2, 1999 prompted this tribute, published in the Sep/Oct 1999 issue. This is among the most personally significant of Tartt's periodical contributions.

Context: The issue also carries a short tribute by William Styron (author of Sophie's Choice). The issue's other features include essays by Kevin Wilson and Katherine Clark, and fiction by Brad Watson.

Cover: OA Issue 29 cover image archived at assets/images/magazines/OA-1999-29-cover.jpg. Cover details: Black-and-white photograph by Frank Ockenfels (close-up of a hand holding a shard of glass). Cover price: $5.25 (Canada $6.25). Display until November 1999. Cover lines (right side): "Donna Tartt & William Styron Pay Tribute to Willie Morris" — confirmation from the cover itself that Tartt's tribute appears in this issue. Other cover lines: "John Simpkins Urges Blacks to Reclaim Watermelon," "Tim McLaurin Defends Hazing (Sort of)," "Two Guys Try to Understand the Bonnie & Clyde Festival." Lead cover line: "A Young Writer's Fear of Glass."

Collector significance: Issue 29 (Sep/Oct 1999) is not part of the OA Goods "Donna Tartt Collection" bundle (which covers Issues 2, 6, and 41); Issue 29 must be sourced separately. OA Issue 29 is accessible at: https://oxfordamerican.org/magazine/issue-29-september-october-1999.

**Canonical URL:** https://oxfordamerican.org/magazine/issue-29-september-october-1999 (OA Issue 29 landing page; no individual article page exists on the OA website)
**Text status:** Print only — no individual article page exists on the OA website; the issue TOC and cover text confirm the Tartt contribution but full text requires a physical copy of OA Issue 29.
**Wayback corroboration:** OA Issue 29 landing page TOC archived in the Wayback Machine. Capture saved locally at `assets/sources/archive/wayback/OA-Issue29-1999-TOC-CDX-WB20210419231006.html` (15 KB). The capture renders the contributor list: *"Other contributors include Donna Tartt, William Styron, Roy Blount Jr., Janet Lembke, John T. Edge, Darcey Steinke, and others."* and the TOC tribute pair: *"TRIBUTE — WILLIE MORRIS, 1934–1999 by Donna Tartt — IT CANNOT BE LONG — Remembering a friend. by William Styron."* Tartt's article body was not captured.
**Cover image:** assets/images/magazines/OA-1999-29-cover.jpg (Fig. 6.12 — Oxford American Issue 29, Sep/Oct 1999)

### 6.13 Donna Tartt in conversation with Antonio Monda — *Il Foglio*, 15 September 2025 — "La letteratura come scoperta"

The interview is the most substantive Tartt-bylined primary source on the record between the 2020 Portis tribute (§6.6) and the 2024 *Harper's* "Art and Artifice" essay (§6.5), and the first major Tartt public statement since the *Harper's* / *Reclaiming Art* cluster of 2024.

**Bibliographic record:**

| Field | Data |
|---|---|
| Title | "Donna Tartt, la letteratura come scoperta" ("Donna Tartt, literature as discovery") |
| Subtitle / framing | *il colloquio* (the conversation) |
| Author / interviewer | **Antonio Monda** (Italian critic, NYU professor, founding artistic director of the Rome Film Festival; longtime literary interviewer) |
| Format | Long-form interview / Q&A in Italian (Tartt's English-language answers translated for publication) |
| Publication | *Il Foglio*, Italian daily newspaper, Cultura section |
| Date | 15 September 2025; "Ultimo aggiornamento: 07:35 AM" |
| Canonical URL | https://www.ilfoglio.it/cultura/2025/09/15/news/donna-tartt-la-letteratura-come-scoperta-8087457/ (alt: -119927) |
| Length | ~2,500 words of Tartt's quoted answers across 10 questions |
| Photo | Getty Images photo of Tartt (caption: "foto Getty") |
| Broader project | Likely tied to Monda's "Incontri ravvicinati" series (Mondadori, 2024) — a volume collecting his interviews with 150 figures from cinema and literature |

**Tartt-authored statements from the interview:**

- **Current writing status (2025).** Tartt has sold her New York apartment and retreated to a ranch in Virginia, "not far from where her mother lives," to write her fourth novel. "Per quel che ricordo non c'è stato un momento nel quale ho deciso di diventare una scrittrice" — "As far as I can remember there wasn't a moment when I decided to become a writer."
- **Bennington study.** "**Bennington, exclusive college del Vermont dove aveva deciso di studiare filosofia mentre Bret Easton Ellis e Jay McInerney approfondivano lo studio della letteratura**" — Tartt states she chose to study **philosophy** at Bennington while Ellis and McInerney studied literature.
- **Childhood-reading list.** *"Lewis Carroll, J.M. Barrie, Mark Twain, Robert Louis Stevenson, George MacDonald, A.A. Milne, Rudyard Kipling, i libri di Oz di Frank Baum, gli Sherlock Holmes di Conan Doyle e le fiabe di Andrew Lang."* These ten figures opened the door for her later to Dickens, Hawthorne, and Edgar Allan Poe. Her mother and great-grandmother ("la mia bisnonna") read aloud to her. Corroborates the reading-aloud detail in §7.19 (Fairy Tale Review Blue Issue 2005) and §7.24 (Burningham *When We Were Young* 2004 hummingbird memoir).
- **On AI in her own work.** *"Non sono sicura come potrebbe essere utile per uno scrittore di narrativa e in assoluto per me"* — Tartt is "not sure how [AI] could be useful for a fiction writer or for me at all."
- **AI as societal risk.** Calls AI primarily a profit-extraction tool for "companies and anyone who can make a fortune plagiarizing intellectual property," and cites risks "for the government, for the environment, for the economy, for the artists from whom voices have been taken without their knowledge or consent." Cites her friend, novelist **John Darnielle** (Mountain Goats / *Wolf in White Van*): *"Abbiamo perso tutto, ma almeno abbiamo dei testi preveggenti"* ("We've lost everything, but at least we have prescient texts").
- **Literature vs cinema.** Tartt argues literature's distinctive power is interiority and "radical empathy" — quotes Shelley's *Defense of Poetry* ("A man, to be greatly good, must imagine intensely and comprehensively"). Cites George Bernard Shaw on *Little Dorrit* being "a more seditious book than *Capital*."
- **On adaptation fidelity.** "Depends on the text and the quality of the filmmaker"; the more intricate / abstract / intimate a text, the more it must be altered to translate to cinema.
- **Modernist visual culture.** Cites T.S. Eliot's "heap of broken images" and Joan Didion on narrative imposition; identifies **William S. Burroughs** as the rare 20th-c writer who anticipated the present screen-cut-up culture.
- **On the liberal/conservative binary.** Suggests these terms have become "slippery and elastic," used "to lash our anger and keep us excited and separated in our algorithmically-curated small fragmented worlds."
- **On populism.** Closes by quoting Plato (*Republic*): "The people will vote for whoever promises the most, and in doing so will surrender their freedom."

**Cross-references for catalog work:**

- The Bennington-philosophy clarification updates §1, §11 (where the prior "classics and philosophy" formulation appears).
- John Darnielle (b. 1967, novelist of *Wolf in White Van* 2014, *Universal Harvester* 2017, *Devil House* 2022; The Mountain Goats frontman) joins the documented Tartt-friend list (a future §10 association-copies / literary-network table).
- The Virginia-ranch residence detail updates earlier §7.25 / §10 references where Tartt's residence was listed as "Virginia and New York City" (per the 2004 *When We Were Young* contributor bio).
- The "great-grandmother read aloud" detail in §7.19 / §7.24 is now triangulated with the mother also reading aloud (per Il Foglio 2025).

**Text status:** Full text saved to `assets/sources/archive/IlFoglio-2025-09-15-Tartt-LaLetteraturaComeScoperta-Monda-interview.html` (479 KB; full article + Tartt's Q&A).

### 6.14 "Murder & Imagination" — Oxford American, Issue 11, Spring 1996 (Special Crime Issue)

**Cover:** *[cover image not yet archived locally — Oxford American Issue 11]*

Recorded from Oxford American's own archival TOC for Issue 11 (`https://oxfordamerican.org/magazine/issue-11-spring-1996`), archived locally at `assets/sources/archive/OA-Issue11-Spring1996-TOC-userArchive-20260527.webarchive` (paired extracted HTML at `OA-Issue11-Spring1996-TOC-userArchive-20260527.html`).

**Bibliographic record:**

| Field | Data |
|---|---|
| Title | "Murder & Imagination" |
| Author | Donna Tartt |
| Section classification | FEATURES (per OA Issue 11 TOC) |
| Issue framing | **Special Crime Issue** — entire issue thematically devoted to crime |
| Publication | *The Oxford American*, Issue 11 |
| Date | Spring 1996 |
| Canonical URL | https://oxfordamerican.org/magazine/issue-11-spring-1996 (Issue 11 landing page; no individual article page on OA website) |

**Issue framing (verbatim from the archived OA editor's letter, lightly excerpted):**

> "I hope this Special Issue of the *Oxford American*, in some little way at least, brings into focus something of the true horror and size of America's crime problem. Much of what we examine in these pages is nightmarish, but when you look at crime . . . you don't get it any other way." — Editor's Letter

The Spring 1996 issue is a **thematic Special Issue on crime** — every column, feature, fiction piece, and end piece engages with the topic. Tartt's "Murder & Imagination" is one of six FEATURES anchoring the issue, positioned alongside:

- "Memories of a Dead Man Walking" by **Sister Helen Prejean**
- **"Murder & Imagination" by Donna Tartt**
- "In Defense of Southern Womanhood: How to Kill Your Man and Walk" by Julia Reed
- "The Chief of Charleston: Reuben Greenberg" by Paige Williams
- "Evil Hearts: The Kentucky Race Murder of 1995" by Dennis Covington
- "Death Row Portraits" by John Ramsey Miller

Adjacent contributors elsewhere in the issue include Florence King ("The Failed Southern Lady: Poor Bleeding Bohemia"), Hal Crowther ("Unsafe at Any Speed"), Fred Chappell ("Salvation in Order"), Richard Bausch ("The Writing Life: On Creating Violence"), Stephanie Zacharek ("Southern Books: White Man's Burden"), Allison Glock ("Confessionals: Bad Girls"), and Steve Yarbrough (End Piece: "On Stealing"). Short stories by Clyde Edgerton ("Send Me to the Electric Chair"), Tom Paine ("Scar Vegas"), and Brad Watson ("Kindred Spirits"). The single poem in the issue is J.E. Pitts's "Fife the Finisher."

**Probable nature of piece:** Given the editor's framing (crime as moral and aesthetic subject) and the FEATURES positioning between Sister Helen Prejean's *Dead Man Walking* memoir-piece and Julia Reed's "In Defense of Southern Womanhood," "Murder & Imagination" is likely Tartt's most substantive published essay on the moral and literary dimensions of murder as a writerly subject — territory adjacent to *The Secret History* (1992), her poem "True Crime" (OA Issue 4, Winter 1994, §7.5), and her *Murder For Love* anthology contribution (Penzler, February 1996, §7.5). The Spring 1996 timing places it in approximately the same month as *Murder For Love*'s February 1, 1996 publication. The Bausch "On Creating Violence" essay and the Edgerton "Send Me to the Electric Chair" story round out a small Spring-1996 cluster of writers-on-violence material.

**Cultural context:** Sister Helen Prejean's *Dead Man Walking* (book 1993; film November 1995; Susan Sarandon won the Best Actress Oscar on March 25, 1996, same quarter as Issue 11's Spring 1996 publication). Issue 11 was Oxford American's editorial response to that cultural moment, with Tartt's contribution at its center.

**Collector significance:**
- **Seventh confirmed Tartt Oxford American piece** (joining §6.1 Basketball Season Issue 2 1992; §7.5 True Crime poem Issue 4 Winter 1994; §6.9 In Melbourne Issue 6 Mar/Apr 1995; §6.12 Willie Morris tribute Issue 29 Sep/Oct 1999; §6.3 Spirituality in the Modern Novel Issue 30 Nov/Dec 1999; §6.8 Spanish Grandeur in Mississippi Issue 41 Fall 2001).
- Falls in the long TSH→TLF composition gap (1992–2002). Tartt's documented mid-1990s output is otherwise: Tam-O'-Shanter (NYer Apr 1993, §5.2), A Christmas Pageant (Harper's Dec 1993, §5.3), Team Spirit (Harper's Apr 1994, §6.2), A Garter Snake (GQ May 1995, §5.4), In Melbourne (OA Mar/Apr 1995, §6.9), True Crime poem (OA Winter 1994 + Murder For Love Feb 1996, §7.5). "Murder & Imagination" appears in the same quarter as *Murder For Love* (Feb 1996) and may be the prose companion to her poem.
- Issue 11 is not part of the OA Goods "Donna Tartt Collection" bundle (which covers Issues 2, 6, and 41 only — Issue 11 must be sourced separately).
- Also of interest to Helen Prejean / *Dead Man Walking* collectors and to Julia Reed completists (Reed died July 2020).
- Not currently catalogued by ABAA dealers; OA back-issue dealers and the OA Goods archive itself are the primary acquisition channels.

**Canonical URL:** https://oxfordamerican.org/magazine/issue-11-spring-1996 (OA Issue 11 landing page; no individual article page exists on the OA website)
**Text status:** Print only — no individual article page exists on the OA website (OA's modern site is a client-rendered SPA and individual archive articles from 1990s issues are not indexed); the issue TOC confirms the Tartt contribution but full text requires a physical copy of OA Issue 11.
**Archive of record:** `assets/sources/archive/OA-Issue11-Spring1996-TOC-userArchive-20260527.webarchive` (Safari binary plist; ~12 MB) + paired `.html` extraction (~392 KB) — both contain the full issue TOC and editor's letter excerpt.
**Acquisition target:** Oxford American Issue 11, Spring 1996 — physical copy needed for full text, page range, and column subtitle.
## 7. Introductions, contributions, and audiobooks Tartt narrated

### 7.1 Introduction: Charles Portis, True Grit (Bloomsbury reissue, 2005)

**Covers:**
- **CON-001** — True Grit (Bloomsbury 2005) - Tartt introduction: `assets/images/_retrieved/CON-001-truegrit-bloomsbury.jpg`
- **CON-001b** — True Grit (Overlook 2010 reissue): `assets/images/_retrieved/CON-001b-publisher.jpg`


Tartt wrote a substantive introduction to Bloomsbury's 2005 UK reissue (published January 17, 2005). The same essay was carried forward in the Overlook Press (US) 2010 movie tie-in edition (ISBN 9781590204597) as an afterword. [86]

**Guardian pre-publication excerpt (January 8, 2005):** An edited version of this introduction was published in The Guardian nine days before the Bloomsbury reissue, under the title "How Mattie got her man" (The Guardian, Saturday, January 8, 2005, pages G6-03 and G7-03). Section: "6 Lives & letters / Rereadings." Full headline: "Donna Tartt on True Grit, a modern classic of one girl's quest to avenge her father's murder / How Mattie got her man." Pull quote: "No living Southern writer captures the spoken idioms of the South as Portis does." The Guardian's own footnote on p. G7-03 reads: "This is an edited version of Donna Tartt's introduction to a new edition of True Grit, published by Bloomsbury on January 17. To order a copy please see the details of the Guardian book service on page 20." Primary scans: `assets/articles/Guardian_2005-01-08_HowMattieGotHerMan_G6-03.pdf` and `assets/articles/Guardian_2005-01-08_HowMattieGotHerMan_G7-03.pdf`.

The Guardian, Saturday January 8, 2005 is the earliest print publication of this essay text (as an edited excerpt), predating the Bloomsbury reissue by nine days. The full, unedited introduction appears in the Bloomsbury book (pub. January 17, 2005) and the Overlook Press (US) 2010 movie tie-in (ISBN 9781590204597).

**Canonical URL (full introduction text):** https://brickmag.com/the-great-abiding-pleasure-of-true-grit/ (Brick Magazine #76, 2005 — full introduction text, freely archived; title in Brick: "The Great, Abiding Pleasure of True Grit"). Guardian excerpt ("How Mattie got her man," Jan 8, 2005): https://www.theguardian.com/books/2005/jan/08/classics.charlieportis (held in the Newspapers.com archive).

**Text status:** Full introduction — FREE (Brick Magazine online archive). Guardian excerpt (edited version) — not accessible online; primary newspaper scans held locally.

**Overlook 2010 reissue:** Full text of the essay collates from `assets/sources/archive/TrueGrit-Portis-OverlookPress-2010Reprint-9781585673698-AnnasArchive.epub`. In the Overlook edition the essay is positioned as the **AFTERWORD** (TOC heading: "AFTERWORD"; opening byline: "DONNA TARTT"; placed after the seven novel sections, consistent with the §7.1 note that Bloomsbury's "introduction" was carried forward by Overlook as an "afterword"). **Length: ~2,915 words.** The Bloomsbury 2005 introduction text and the Overlook 2010 afterword text are the same essay in two structural positions across two imprints.

**Structural outline of Tartt's essay (six discernible movements within the ~2,915 words):**

1. **What it means to "love" a book.** Opens with the distinction between books one read once long ago, books one knows by hearsay, and the smaller class of books one returns to repeatedly across a lifetime. Argues *True Grit* belongs to the third category and is therefore a masterpiece.
2. **First encounters with the novel.** Discusses her family's relationship with the book — her mother kept losing and replacing copies because friends never returned them; in one episode her mother borrowed the library's copy and pretended to have lost it. Recounts reading *True Grit* as part of Honors English alongside Whitman, Hawthorne, and Poe at her Mississippi school.
3. **Mattie Ross as narrator.** Detailed close reading of Mattie's voice — its blend of biblical formality, Presbyterian moralism, frontier vernacular, and quiet humor. Treats Mattie as a unique American narrative voice rivalling Huck Finn.
4. **Rooster Cogburn / Marshal Cogburn portrait.** The novel's transformative achievement, per Tartt, is to take a stock figure (the drunken one-eyed federal marshal) and make him morally complex; argues the John Wayne film flattens this.
5. **Southern/Western boundary location.** *True Grit*'s geographical-temporal setting is "where chivalry meets the frontier — where the old Confederacy starts to merge and shade away into the Wild West." The book closes at a travelling Wild West show in early-1900s Memphis: "at once in the twentieth century and firmly enshrined in myth and legend."
6. **Publication history and rediscovery.** Cites Roald Dahl's 1968 review ("the best novel to come my way in a long time… Since what? What book has given me greater pleasure in the last five years? Or in the last twenty?"). Closes with the rationale for the reissue and the Tartt-as-evangelist framing for introducing the novel to a new generation of readers.

**Key Tartt-authored line (canonical pull-quote, also used by Guardian as their headline):** *"No living Southern writer captures the spoken idioms of the South as Portis does."*

**Other notable passages:**
- *"I think it goes without saying that most books that engage readers on this very high level are masterpieces; and this is why I believe that True Grit by Charles Portis is a masterpiece."* (opening framing)
- The mother-borrowing-the-library-copy anecdote is a memorable autobiographical disclosure; useful for §3-cross-reference to Tartt's biographical-Mississippi material
- The Roald Dahl quotation is a useful secondary citation for any TG reception history

**Cross-references:**
- The *Fairy Tale Review* Blue Issue 2005 essay (§7.19, "On Barrie and Stevenson") independently confirms Tartt's lifelong relationship with the book — her great-grandmother had read *True Grit* aloud to her in early childhood, which provides the deep biographical context for this 2005 introduction
- The 2020 NYTBR Portis tribute (§6.11) revisits many of these themes after Portis's death in February 2020 — usefully read as the bookend to the 2005 essay
- The Overlook 2010 reissue (ISBN 9781585673698) appears to share the standardized Overlook editioning ISBN with the 2010 movie tie-in (ISBN 9781590204597 noted earlier in this entry)

**File:** `assets/sources/archive/TrueGrit-Portis-OverlookPress-2010Reprint-9781585673698-AnnasArchive.epub` (full text of the Tartt afterword sits in `text/part0013.html` within the EPUB).
